# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Django ORM Raw SQL Injection Prevention

## Your Task

Find all Python source files in django/django that handle raw SQL queries and prevent SQL injection: the RawSQL expression sanitization, the raw() queryset method parameter binding, and the cursor.execute() parameterization enforcement.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the security domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/django--674eda1c.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/django--674eda1c` (django/django)

{"content":"91: \n92: .. _sql-injection-protection:\n93: \n94: SQL injection protection\n95: ========================\n96: \n97: SQL injection is a type of attack where a malicious user is able to execute\n98: arbitrary SQL code on a database. This can result in records\n99: being deleted or data leakage.\n100: \n101: Django's querysets are protected from SQL injection since their queries are\n102: constructed using query parameterization. A query's SQL code is defined\n103: separately from the query's parameters. Since parameters may be user-provided\n104: and therefore unsafe, they are escaped by the underlying database driver.\n105: \n106: Django also gives developers power to write :ref:`raw queries\n107: <executing-raw-queries>` or execute :ref:`custom sql <executing-custom-sql>`.\n108: These capabilities should be used sparingly and you should always be careful to\n109: properly escape any parameters that the user can control. Perfect! I've completed a comprehensive analysis of Django's SQL injection prevention mechanisms for raw SQL queries. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Key Findings

I've identified **13 source files** and **18 key symbols** that implement Django's SQL injection prevention:

### Core Components:

1. **RawSQL Expression** (expressions.py:1236) - Enforces separation of SQL from parameters
2. **raw() QuerySet Method** (query.py:1461) - Creates parameterized raw queries
3. **RawQuery Class** (sql/query.py:155) - Core parameterized query execution with `_execute_query()` method
4. **CursorWrapper** (backends/utils.py:17) - Generic PEP-249 parameterized execution wrapper
5. **Parameter Adaptation** (base/operations.py:550) - Converts types without modifying SQL
6. **Backend-Specific Implementations** - MySQL, SQLite, Oracle, and PostgreSQL cursor wrappers

### Prevention Mechanism:

Django prevents SQL injection through **PEP-249 parameterized query execution**:
- SQL code and parameters are kept **completely separate** throughout execution
- **No string interpolation** is used to insert parameters
- Parameters are passed to `cursor.execute(sql, params)` where the **database driver escapes them**
- Type conversion happens safely without modifying the SQL string

The answer.json file has been created at `/workspace/answer.json` with complete documentation of all relevant files, symbols, the execution chain, and a detailed narrative explanation of how Django's SQL injection prevention works.

For example:\\n18: \\n19:     * The :doc:`QuerySet API </ref/models/querysets>` is extensive.\\n20:     * You can :meth:`annotate <.QuerySet.annotate>` and :doc:`aggregate\\n21:       </topics/db/aggregation>` using many built-in :doc:`database functions\\n22:       </ref/models/database-functions>`. Beyond those, you can create\\n23:       :doc:`custom query expressions </ref/models/expressions/>`.\\n24: \\n25:     Before using raw SQL, explore :doc:`the ORM </topics/db/index>`. Ask on\\n26:     one of :doc:`the support channels </faq/help>` to see if the ORM supports\\n27:     your use case.\\n28: \\n29: .. warning::\\n30: \\n31:     You should be very careful whenever you write raw SQL. Every time you use\\n32:     it, you should properly escape any parameters that the user can control\\n33:     by using ``params`` in order to protect against SQL injection attacks.\\n34:     Please read more about :ref:`SQL injection protection\\n35:     <sql-injection-protection>`.\\n36: \\n37: .. _executing-raw-queries:\\n38: \\n39: Performing raw queries\\n40: ======================\\n41: \\n42: The ``raw()`` manager method can be used to perform raw SQL queries that\\n43: return model instances:\\n44: \\n45: .. method:: Manager.raw(raw_query, params=(), translations=None)\\n46: \\n47: This method takes a raw SQL query, executes it, and returns a\\n48: ``django.db.models.query.RawQuerySet`` instance. This ``RawQuerySet`` instance\\n49: can be iterated over like a normal :class:`~django.db.models.query.QuerySet` to\\n50: provide object instances.\\n51: \\n52: This is best illustrated with an example. Suppose you have the following\\n53: model::\\n54: \\n55:     class Person(models.Model):\\n56:         first_name = models.CharField(...)\\n57:         last_name = models.CharField(...)\\n58:         birth_date = models.DateField(...)\\n59: \\n60: You could then execute custom SQL like so:\\n61: \\n62: .. code-block:: pycon\\n63: \\n64:     >>> for p in Person.objects.raw(\\\"SELECT * FROM myapp_person\\\"):\\n65:     ...     print(p)\\n66:     ...\\n67:     John Smith\\n68:     Jane Jones\\n69: \\n70: This example isn't very exciting -- it's exactly the same as running\\n71: ``Person.objects.all()``. In the example\\n81:     we've assumed that the ``Person`` model lives in an app named ``myapp``,\\n82:     so its table would be ``myapp_person``.\\n83: \\n84:     For more details check out the documentation for the\\n85:     :attr:`~Options.db_table` option, which also lets you manually set the\\n86:     database table name.\\n87: \\n88: .. warning::\\n89: \\n90:     No checking is done on the SQL statement that is passed in to ``.raw()``.\\n91:     Django expects that the statement will return a set of rows from the\\n92:     database, but does nothing to enforce that. For example, we could use `PostgreSQL's age() function`__ to get a list\\n203: of people with their ages calculated by the database:\\n204: \\n205: .. code-block:: pycon\\n206: \\n207:     >>> people = Person.objects.raw(\\\"SELECT *, age(birth_date) AS age FROM myapp_person\\\")\\n208:     >>> for p in people:\\n209:     ...     print(\\\"%s is %s.\\\" % (p.first_name, p.age))\\n210:     ...\\n211:     John is 37.\\n212:     Jane is 42.\\n213:     ...\\n214: \\n215: You can often avoid using raw SQL to compute annotations by instead using a\\n216: :ref:`Func() expression <func-expressions>`.\\n217: \\n218: __ https://www.postgresql.org/docs/current/functions-datetime.html\\n219: \\n220: Passing parameters into ``raw()``\\n221: ---------------------------------\\n222: \\n223: If you need to perform parameterized queries, you can use the ``params``\\n224: argument to ``raw()``:\\n225: \\n226: .. code-block:: pycon\\n227: \\n228:     >>> lname = \\\"Doe\\\"\\n229:     >>> Person.objects.raw(\\\"SELECT * FROM myapp_person WHERE last_name = %s\\\", [lname])\\n230: \\n231: ``params`` is a list or dictionary of parameters. You'll use ``%s``\\n232: placeholders in the query string for a list, or ``%(key)s``\\n233: placeholders for a dictionary (where ``key`` is replaced by a\\n234: dictionary key), regardless of your database engine. Such placeholders will be\\n235: replaced with parameters from the ``params`` argument.\\n236: \\n237: .. note::\\n238: \\n239:    Dictionary params are not supported with the SQLite backend; with\\n240:    this backend, you must pass parameters as a list.\\n241: \\n242: .. warning::\\n243: \\n244:     **Do not use string formatting on raw queries or quote placeholders in your\\n245:     SQL strings!**\\n246: \\n247:     It's tempting to write the above query as:\\n248: \\n249:     .. code-block:: pycon\\n250: \\n251:         >>> query = \\\"SELECT * FROM myapp_person WHERE last_name = %s\\\" % lname\\n252:         >>> Person.objects.raw(query)\\n253: \\n254:     You might also think you should write your query like this (with quotes\\n255:     around ``%s``):\\n256: \\n257:     .. code-block:: pycon\\n258: \\n259:         >>> query = \\\"SELECT * FROM myapp_person WHERE last_name = '%s'\\\"\\n260: \\n261:     **Don't make either of these mistakes.**\\n262: \\n263:     As discussed in :ref:`sql-injection-protection`, using the ``params``\\n264:     argument and leaving the placeholders unquoted protects you from `SQL\\n265:     injection attacks`__, a common exploit where attackers inject arbitrary\\n266:     SQL into your database. If you use string interpolation or quote the\\n267:     placeholder, you're at risk for SQL injection.\\n268: \\n269: __ https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SQL_injection\\n270: \\n271: .. _executing-custom-sql:\\n272: \\n273: Executing custom SQL directly\\n274: =============================\\n275: \\n276: Sometimes even :meth:`Manager.raw` isn't quite enough: you might need to\\n277: perform queries that don't map cleanly to models, or directly execute\\n278: ``UPDATE``, ``INSERT``, or ``DELETE`` queries.\\n279: \\n280: In these cases, you can always access the database directly, routing around\\n281: the model layer entirely.\\n282: \\n283: The object ``django.db.connection`` represents the default database\\n284: connection. To use the database connection, call ``connection.cursor()`` to\\n285: get a cursor object. Then, call ``cursor.execute(sql, [params])`` to execute\\n286: the SQL and ``cursor.fetchone()`` or ``cursor.fetchall()`` to return the\\n287: resulting rows.\\n288: \\n289: For example::\\n290: \\n291:     from django.db import connection\\n292: \\n293: \\n294:     def my_custom_sql(self):\\n295:         with connection.cursor() as cursor:\\n296:             cursor.execute(\\\"UPDATE bar SET foo = 1 WHERE baz = %s\\\", [self.baz])\\n297:             cursor.execute(\\\"SELECT foo FROM bar WHERE baz = %s\\\", [self.baz])\\n298:             row = cursor.fetchone()\\n299: \\n300:         return row\\n301: \\n302: To protect against SQL injection, you must not include quotes around the ``%s``\\n303: placeholders in the SQL string.\\n304: \\n305: Note that if you want to include literal percent signs in the query, you have\\n306: to double them in the case you are passing parameters::\\n307: \\n308:      cursor.execute(\\\"SELECT foo FROM bar WHERE baz = '30%'\\\")\\n309:      cursor.execute(\\\"SELECT foo FROM bar WHERE baz = '30%%' AND id = %s\\\", [self.id])\\n310: \\n311: If you are using :doc:`more than one database </topics/db/multi-db>`, you can\\n312: use ``django.db.connections`` to obtain the connection (and cursor) for a\\n313: specific database. ``django.db.connections`` is a dictionary-like\\n314: object that allows you to retrieve a specific connection using its\\n315: alias::\\n316: \\n317:     from django.db import connections\\n318: \\n319:     with connections[\\\"my_db_alias\\\"].cursor() as cursor:\\n320:         # Your code here\\n321:         ...\\n322: \\n323: By default, the Python DB API will return results without their field names,\\n324: which means you end up with a ``list`` of values, rather than a ``dict``. At a\\n325: small performance and memory cost, you can return results as a ``dict`` by\\n326: using something like this::\\n327: \\n328:     def dictfetchall(cursor):\\n329:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n330:         Return all rows from a cursor as a dict.\\n331:         Assume the column names are unique.\\n332:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n333:         columns = [col[0] for col in cursor.description]\\n334:         return [dict(zip(columns, row)) for row in cursor.fetchall()]\\n335: \\n336: Another option is to use :func:`collections.namedtuple` from the Python\\n337: standard library. A ``namedtuple`` is a tuple-like object that has fields\\n338: accessible by attribute lookup; it's also indexable and iterable. Results are\\n339: immutable and accessible by field names or indices, which might be useful::\\n340: \\n341:     from collections import namedtuple\\n342: \\n343: \\n344:     def namedtuplefetchall(cursor):\\n345:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n346:         Return all rows from a cursor as a namedtuple.\\n347:         Assume the column names are unique.\\n348:         \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n349:         desc = cursor.description\\n350:         nt_result = namedtuple(\\\"Result\\\", [col[0] for col in desc])\\n351: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/django/django@202eca7795710c82cd64a248650afc64f905a17e/-/blob/docs/topics/db/sql.txt%3FL200-350\"}",

This can result in records\\n99: being deleted or data leakage.\\n100: \\n101: Django's querysets are protected from SQL injection since their queries are\\n102: constructed using query parameterization. A query's SQL code is defined\\n103: separately from the query's parameters. Since parameters may be user-provided\\n104: and therefore unsafe, they are escaped by the underlying database driver.\\n105: \\n106: Django also gives developers power to write :ref:`raw queries\\n107: <executing-raw-queries>` or execute :ref:`custom sql <executing-custom-sql>`.\\n108: These capabilities should be used sparingly and you should always be careful to\\n109: properly escape any parameters that the user can control. In addition, you\\n110: should exercise caution when using\\n111: :meth:`~django.db.models.query.QuerySet.extra` and\\n112: :class:`~django.db.models.expressions.RawSQL`.\\n113: \\n114: Clickjacking protection\\n115: =======================\\n116: \\n117: Clickjacking is a type of attack where a malicious site wraps another site\\n118: in a frame. This attack can result in an unsuspecting user being tricked\\n119: into performing unintended actions on the target site.\\n120: \\n121: Django contains :ref:`clickjacking protection <clickjacking-prevention>` in the\\n122: form of the :mod:`X-Frame-Options middleware\\n123: <django.middleware.clickjacking.XFrameOptionsMiddleware>` which in a supporting\\n124: browser can prevent a site from being rendered inside a frame. It is possible\\n125: to disable the protection on a per view basis or to configure the exact header\\n126: value sent.\\n127: \\n128: The middleware is strongly recommended for any site that does not need to have\\n129: its pages wrapped in a frame by third party sites, or only needs to allow that\\n130: for a small section of the site.\\n131: \\n132: .. _security-recommendation-ssl:\\n133: \\n134: SSL/HTTPS\\n135: =========\\n136: \\n137: It is always better for security to deploy your site behind HTTPS. Without\\n138: this, it is possible for malicious network users to sniff authentication\\n139: credentials or any other information transferred between client and server, and\\n140: in some cases -- **active** network attackers -- to alter data that is sent in\\n141: either direction.\\n142: \\n143: If you want the protection that HTTPS provides, and have enabled it on your\\n144: server, there are some additional steps you may need:\\n145: \\n146: * If necessary, set :setting:`SECURE_PROXY_SSL_HEADER`, ensuring that you have\\n147:   understood the warnings there thoroughly. Failure to do this can result\\n148:   in CSRF vulnerabilities, and failure to do it correctly can also be\\n149:   dangerous!\\n150: \\n151: * Set :setting:`SECURE_SSL_REDIRECT` to ``True``, so that requests over HTTP\\n152:   are redirected to HTTPS.\\n153: \\n154:   Please note the caveats under :setting:`SECURE_PROXY_SSL_HEADER`. concatenation, or f-strings to insert parameters into SQL\\n3. **Database Driver Escaping**: Parameters are passed to cursor.execute(sql, params) where the database driver's native parameter binding
